                    }
                }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41587"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence of foxtail millet ( Setaria italica ) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Biotechnology 2012 30:6"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2012-05-13"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2012 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Completion of genome sequences for the diploid Setaria italica reveals features of C4 photosynthesis that could enable improvement of the polyploid biofuel crop switchgrass (Panicum virgatum). The genetic basis of biotechnologically relevant traits, including drought tolerance, photosynthetic efficiency and flowering control, is also highlighted. Foxtail millet (Setaria italica), a member of the Poaceae grass family, is an important food and fodder crop in arid regions and has potential for use as a C4 biofuel. It is a model system for other biofuel grasses, including switchgrass and pearl millet. We produced a draft genome (&#8764;423 Mb) anchored onto nine chromosomes and annotated 38,801 genes. Key chromosome reshuffling events were detected through collinearity identification between foxtail millet, rice and sorghum including two reshuffling events fusing rice chromosomes 7 and 9, 3 and 10 to foxtail millet chromosomes 2 and 9, respectively, that occurred after the divergence of foxtail millet and rice, and a single reshuffling event fusing rice chromosome 5 and 12 to foxtail millet chromosome 3 that occurred after the divergence of millet and sorghum. Rearrangements in the C4 photosynthesis pathway were also identified."/>

                    <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">Genome sequence of foxtail millet (<i>Setaria italica</i>) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential</h1>

data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Haitao-Xiang" data-author-popup="auth-Haitao-Xiang">Haitao Xiang</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Foxtail millet (<i>Setaria italica</i>), a member of the Poaceae grass family, is an important food and fodder crop in arid regions and has potential for use as a C<sub>4</sub> biofuel. It is a model system for other biofuel grasses, including switchgrass and pearl millet. We produced a draft genome (<span class="stix">∼</span>423 Mb) anchored onto nine chromosomes and annotated 38,801 genes. Key chromosome reshuffling events were detected through collinearity identification between foxtail millet, rice and sorghum including two reshuffling events fusing rice chromosomes 7 and 9, 3 and 10 to foxtail millet chromosomes 2 and 9, respectively, that occurred after the divergence of foxtail millet and rice, and a single reshuffling event fusing rice chromosome 5 and 12 to foxtail millet chromosome 3 that occurred after the divergence of millet and sorghum. Rearrangements in the C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis pathway were also identified.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Foxtail millet (<i>Setaria italica</i>) was domesticated &gt;8,700 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Zohary, D. &amp; Hopf, M. Domestication of Plants in the Old World: the Origin and Spread of Cultivated Plants in West Asia, Europe, and the Nile Valley. Third edn. (Oxford University Press, 2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d30e834">1</a></sup>. Although foxtail millet was an important foodstuff in neolithic China and continues to be cultivated in semi-arid areas, the specific growth conditions and low productivity have limited its potential as a crop. Foxtail millet is closely related to several biofuel grasses with complex genomes, such as switchgrass (<i>Panicum virgatum</i>), napier grass (<i>Pennisetum purpureum</i>) and pearl millet (<i>Pennisetum glaucum</i>). Of these related species, foxtail millet is the most suitable plant for whole genome sequencing and analysis owing to its small genome (<span class="stix">∼</span>490 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Doust, A.N., Kellogg, E.A., Devos, K.M. &amp; Bennetzen, J.L. Foxtail millet: a sequence-driven grass model system. Plant Physiol. 149, 137–141 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d30e848">2</a></sup>, self-pollination, rich genetic diversity (<span class="stix">∼</span>6,000 varieties)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wang, Z.M., Devos, K.M., Liu, C.J., Wang, R.Q. &amp; Gale, M.D. Construction of RFLP-based maps of foxtail millet, Setaria italica (L.) P. Beauv. Theor. Appl. Genet. 96, 31–36 (1998)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d30e852">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Devos, K.M., Wang, Z.M., Beales, J., Sasaki, T. &amp; Gale, M.D. Comparative genetic maps of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) and rice (Oryza sativa). Theor. Appl. Genet. 96, 63–68 (1998)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d30e855">4</a></sup>, the existence of a complete collection of germplasm and the availability of high-throughput transformation platforms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Doust, A.N., Kellogg, E.A., Devos, K.M. &amp; Bennetzen, J.L. Foxtail millet: a sequence-driven grass model system. Plant Physiol. 149, 137–141 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d30e859">2</a></sup>.</p><p>Genetic maps have already been constructed for foxtail millet using different markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wang, Z.M., Devos, K.M., Liu, C.J., Wang, R.Q. &amp; Gale, M.D. Construction of RFLP-based maps of foxtail millet, Setaria italica (L.) P. Beauv. Theor. Appl. Genet. 96, 31–36 (1998)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d30e866">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Devos, K.M., Wang, Z.M., Beales, J., Sasaki, T. &amp; Gale, M.D. Comparative genetic maps of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) and rice (Oryza sativa). Theor. Appl. Genet. 96, 63–68 (1998)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d30e869">4</a></sup>. Comparisons between genetic maps of foxtail millet, rice and pearl millet have shed light on the chromosomal relationships between these grasses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Devos, K.M., Wang, Z.M., Beales, J., Sasaki, T. &amp; Gale, M.D. Comparative genetic maps of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) and rice (Oryza sativa). Theor. Appl. Genet. 96, 63–68 (1998)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d30e873">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Devos, K.M., Pittaway, T.S., Reynolds, A. &amp; Gale, M.D. Comparative mapping reveals a complex relationship between the pearl millet genome and those of foxtail millet and rice. Theor. Appl. Genet. 100, 190–198 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d30e876">5</a></sup>. Genetic variations of foxtail millet have also been studied in some genomic regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, C. et al. Population genetics of foxtail millet and its wild ancestor. BMC Genet. 11, 90 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d30e880">6</a></sup> and population of foxtail millet has been investigated using single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP), simple sequence repeat and other markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Fukunaga, K., Wang, Z., Kato, K. &amp; Kawase, M. Geographical variation of nuclear genome RFLPs and genetic differentiation in foxtail millet, Setaria italica (L.) P. Beauv. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 49, 95–101 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d30e884">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Schontz, D. &amp; Rether, B. Genetic variability in foxtail millet, Setaria italica (L.) P. Beauv.: Identification and classification of lines with RAPD markers. Plant Breed. 118, 190–192 (1999)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d30e887">8</a></sup>. Evolutionary relationships between foxtail millet and its wild ancestor green millet have been studied using polymorphisms in single genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Fukunaga, K.F., Kawase, M.K. &amp; Kato, K.K. Structural variation in the Waxy gene and differentiation in foxtail millet [Setaria italica (L.) P. Beauv.]: implications for multiple origins of the waxy phenotype. Mol. Genet. Genomics 268, 214–222 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d30e891">9</a></sup> or genomic regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, C. et al. Population genetics of foxtail millet and its wild ancestor. BMC Genet. 11, 90 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d30e896">6</a></sup>. Small collections of other resources, including simple sequence repeats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jia, X.-P. Development of EST-SSR in foxtail millet (Setaria italica). Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 54, 233–236 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d30e900">10</a></sup> and expressed sequence tags (ESTs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Zhang, J. et al. Construction and application of EST library from Setaria italica in response to dehydration stress. Genomics 90, 121–131 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d30e904">11</a></sup>, have also been developed. The process of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis and genes in C4 pathway has been investigated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Langdale, J.A. C4 cycles: past, present, and future research on C4 photosynthesis. Plant Cell 23, 3879–3892 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d30e910">12</a></sup>. The availability of genome sequences has enabled the identification of gene duplication and neofunctionalization events that have contributed to the evolution of several enzymes involved in C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Monson, R.K. Gene Duplication, Neofunctionalization, and the Evolution of C4 Photosynthesis. Int. J. Plant Sci. 164, S43–S54 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d30e917">13</a></sup>. Carboxylase, which catalyses the first reaction in C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis, is thought to differ in C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ludwig, M. Carbonic anhydrase and the molecular evolution of C4 photosynthesis. Plant Cell Environ. 35, 22–37 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d30e927">14</a></sup>. However, evolution of carboxylase genes in C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> plants is still not well understood, especially in grasses. Using the foxtail millet genome draft, we were able to analyze carboxylase gene evolution in C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> grasses.</p><p>We used whole genome shotgun combined with next-generation sequencing to assemble a draft genome of the foxtail millet strain 'Zhang gu'. The final genome assembly was 423 Mb, <span class="stix">∼</span>86% of the estimated genome size, with repeats comprising <span class="stix">∼</span>46% of the genome. We annotated 38,801 protein-coding genes, of which <span class="stix">∼</span>81% were expressed. Nine foxtail millet chromosomes were found to be formed after three chromosomal reshuffling events. The carbonic anhydrase genes in the C<sub>4</sub> pathway were found to differ between C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> grasses. Finally, using developed structural variant and SNP markers, we mapped the sethoxydim-resistance trait to a known gene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Délye, C., Wang, T. &amp; Darmency, H. An isoleucine-leucine substitution in chloroplastic acetyl-CoA carboxylase from green foxtail (Setaria viridis L. Beauv.) is responsible for resistance to the cyclohexanedione herbicide sethoxydim. Planta 214, 421–427 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d30e949">15</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Whole genome shotgun combined with next-generation sequencing has been used to assemble large mammal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d30e965">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Kim, E.B. et al. Genome sequencing reveals insights into physiology and longevity of the naked mole rat. Nature 479, 223–227 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d30e968">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Xu, X. et al. The genomic sequence of the Chinese hamster ovary (CHO)-K1 cell line. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 735–741 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d30e971">18</a></sup> and plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Xu, X. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d30e975">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035–1039 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d30e978">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275–1281 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d30e981">21</a></sup>. Although this method has proven limitations when used to assemble long-repeat regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Alkan, C., Sajjadian, S. &amp; Eichler, E.E. Limitations of next-generation genome sequence assembly. Nat. Methods 8, 61–65 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d30e985">22</a></sup>, improvements in sequencing (using paired-end sequencing) and bioinformatics (more efficient assemblers)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d30e989">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513–1518 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d30e992">24</a></sup> have enabled resolution of repeat-rich regions from such data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Schatz, M.C., Delcher, A.L. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. Assembly of large genomes using second-generation sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 1165–1173 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d30e996">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zhang, J., Chiodini, R., Badr, A. &amp; Zhang, G. The impact of next-generation sequencing on genomics. J. Genet. Genomics 38, 95–109 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d30e999">26</a></sup>. We used the whole genome shotgun–next-generation sequencing strategy to assemble the draft genome of a widely cultivated strain of foxtail millet named Zhang gu. DNA libraries with different insert sizes ranging from 170 bp to 40 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 1</a>) were cloned to resolve sequences of repetitive regions. Sequencing was carried out using Illumina second-generation sequencers. After filtering, <span class="stix">∼</span>40 Gb data were subjected to SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d30e1007">23</a></sup> which used <i>de Bruijn</i> graphs for genome assembly using pair-end short-reads. After gap filling, the contig N50 was 25.4 kb, and 90% (in length) of the contigs were contained in 16,903 contigs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The scaffold N50 was 1.0 Mb and 90% of the scaffolds (380 Mb) were contained in the 439 longest scaffolds. The total length of all scaffolds was 423 Mb, with 28 Mb (6.6%) gaps . The genome size was estimated to be <span class="stix">∼</span>490 Mb by cytogenetic methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Doust, A.N., Kellogg, E.A., Devos, K.M. &amp; Bennetzen, J.L. Foxtail millet: a sequence-driven grass model system. Plant Physiol. 149, 137–141 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d30e1020">2</a></sup>, which was consistent with the estimation by k-mer analysis carried out in this study (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>) (<span class="stix">∼</span>485 Mb). Thus the scaffolds covered <span class="stix">∼</span>86% (81% if excluding gaps) of the total genome.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of genome assembly and annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nbt.2195/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Marker development and genetic map construction</h3><p>We resequenced another widely cultivated strain of foxtail millet named 'A2' to identify variations and construct a genetic map for anchoring scaffolds onto chromosomes. A2 is a photothermo-sensitive male-sterile line. We used Illumina GA II to sequence the A2 strain to <span class="stix">∼</span>10× in depth. We identified 542,322 SNPs, 33,587 small insertion and deletions (indels), and 10,839 structural variants between A2 and Zhang gu (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). Then we constructed an F<sub>2</sub> population with 480 individuals by crossing Zhang gu and A2. We genotyped 759 variations including 118 SNPs and 641 structural variants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 3</a>) in the F<sub>2</sub> population. Of those markers, the 751 (covering the 613 longest scaffolds) that showed segregation in the F<sub>2</sub> population were used to construct the genetic map. The 751 markers were clustered into nine linkage groups (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>), and we were able to anchor the 613 longest scaffolds (<span class="stix">∼</span>400 Mb, with <span class="stix">∼</span>26 Mb gaps) onto nine chromosomes.</p><p>We compared the genetic distance to the physical distance of these 751 genetic markers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>) and found that 33% of the genome was located in low-recombination regions, a percentage lower than in sorghum (62%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d30e1446">27</a></sup> but higher than in rice (15%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d30e1450">28</a></sup>. The average ratio of genetic-to-physical distance in low- and high-recombination regions was 0.44 cM/Mb and 6.77 cM/Mb, respectively. In the low-recombination regions, we identified a clustered 155-bp repeat unit, which was similar in sequence to the 140-bp centromere elements in sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Murat, F. et al. Ancestral grass karyotype reconstruction unravels new mechanisms of genome shuffling as a source of plant evolution. Genome Res. 20, 1545–1557 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d30e1454">29</a></sup>. This repeat unit is likely to be a constitutive, centromere-related element.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Assessment of genome assembly</h3><p>We first mapped all the individual reads back to the genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 4</a>) and found that &gt;89% of the reads could be mapped, which was consistent with the estimated coverage of the assembly. Among mapped reads, <span class="stix">∼</span>8% were mapped without a proper paired-end relationship, which could be caused by difficulty in mapping or a discrepancy in assembly. We compared our genome assembly with the sorghum genome (version 1.0) to identify 1,937 large-scale rearrangements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5</a>), and most of them (99%) were well supported by paired-end mapped reads; <span class="stix">∼</span>1% was not well supported, which might be because of assembly errors (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). We also used optical mapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Zhou, S. et al. A single molecule scaffold for the maize genome. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000711 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d30e1475">30</a></sup> to validate our genome assembly. We mapped 99.8% of the optical fragments to the assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>), which confirms the accuracy of the genome assembly. Finally we used a different assembler, ALLPATHS-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513–1518 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d30e1483">24</a></sup>, to carry out a second assembly. The two assemblies were comparable (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 2 and 6</a>), although the assembly by ALLPATHS-LG had longer contigs, longer scaffolds, more gaps and shorter total length. We mapped the 20 longest scaffolds assembled by ALLPATHS-LG (123 Mb in total) to our genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>) and found that 99% of these sequences were present in our assembly. Further, structural variations between the two assemblies only comprised <span class="stix">∼</span>0.9% of the mapped length.</p><p>We also assessed the assembly of genes. We sequenced mRNAs from four tissues (root, leaf, spica and stem), assembled the transcripts and mapped them to the genome assembly. Coverage of the gene regions was estimated to be <span class="stix">∼</span>96% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 7</a>). We then assessed coverage of core eukaryotic genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K., Ning, Z., Keane, T. &amp; Korf, I. Assessing the gene space in draft genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 289–297 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d30e1499">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d30e1502">32</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 8</a>), which showed that &gt;99% of conserved genes were present. We also mapped all the available gene sequences from foxtail millet present in the NCBI nucleotide database (28 genes) to the genome assembly, and of these, 27 genes were present in our draft genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 9</a>). Finally, we mapped genes from <i>Arabidopsis</i>, sorghum, maize and rice to the genome to identify incomplete gene fragments (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a>). We randomly selected 82 gene fragments, and 81 (99%) of them were validated by Sanger sequencing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 11</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Repeat content in the draft genome</h3><p>Comprehensive repeat annotation of the draft Zhang gu genome revealed that <span class="stix">∼</span>46% of the genome comprised transposable elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 12</a>). Both retroelements (class I transposable element; 31.6%) and DNA transposons (class II transposable element; 9.4%) were identified. The most abundant repeat elements were long terminal repeats (LTRs), which comprised 29.6% of the genome. Of these LTRs, 22.1% of the genome was <i>gypsy</i> and 7.2% of the genome was <i>copia</i> (the rest 0.3% were other LTRs), with the ratio of <i>gypsy</i>-like to <i>copia</i>-like elements 3.1:1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 13</a>). Other types of transposable elements were DNA transposons (9.4%), long interspersed nuclear elements (LINEs, 1.8%), short interspersed nuclear elements (SINEs, 0.2%) and other types (0.11%). There were 5.4% uncharacterized repeats. Transposable elements were more abundant in the low-recombination and gene-poor regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). The insertion time of most LTRs was estimated to be <span class="stix">∼</span>0.3–1.0 million years ago (Myr ago) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Recent LTRs (&lt;0.1 Myr ago) comprised only 4.7% of the total LTRs. Distribution of recent LTRs was more random than that of ancient LTRs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>).</p><p>Transposable element proportions in the five grasses (<i>Brachypodium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d30e1566">33</a></sup>, rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d30e1570">28</a></sup>, sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d30e1574">27</a></sup>, maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Schnable, P.S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d30e1578">34</a></sup> and foxtail millet; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 13</a>) varied from 27% (<i>Brachypodium</i>) to 84% (maize), with higher transposable element contents in larger genomes. In the case of LTRs, the proportions present in <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice (<span class="stix">∼</span>20%) and foxtail millet (29.6%) were substantially lower than those present in sorghum (54%) and maize (75%). The composition of LTRs in these species varied, too. The ratio of <i>gypsy</i> LTRs to <i>copia</i> LTRs was highest in sorghum (3.7 to 1), lower in rice (2.83 to 1), <i>Brachypodium</i> (3.3 to 1) and foxtail millet (3.1 to 1), and lowest in maize (2.0 to 1). It has been reported that genome size changes that occur through rapid multiplication of transposable elements probably has large effects on specification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Bennetzen, J.L. Transposable element contributions to plant gene and genome evolution. Plant Mol. Biol. 42, 251–269 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d30e1601">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Hu, T.T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d30e1604">36</a></sup>. Therefore, different transposable element compositions in grasses resulting from transposable element multiplication might have affected the diversification of grasses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Foxtail millet genes</h3><p>We used an integrated annotation pipeline to predict a total of 38,801 genes in the draft genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 14</a>). According to mRNA sequencing data from four tissues, <span class="stix">∼</span>81.7% of these genes were expressed. The average transcript length of annotated genes was 2,522 bp, which was substantially shorter than transcripts predicted by mapping the RNA sequencing data to the genome. To resolve this discrepancy, we added untranslated regions (UTRs) to the gene models, in accordance with the RNA sequencing data. In foxtail millet, the average intron length (442 bp), average exon length (256 bp) and average exons per gene (4.3) were similar to all other grasses except maize (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 15</a>). Functional annotation confirmed that 78.8% of the genes have homologs with known functions in protein databases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 16</a>). We searched for conserved genes present in other grasses in the foxtail millet gene set, and found that 99% (11,847 out of 11,969) of conserved genes have homologs in foxtail millet. This confirmed that the draft sequence is reasonably complete. We also identified 1,367 pseudogenes in the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 17</a>).</p><p>Noncoding RNA genes were predicted in the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 18</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). We identified 99 ribosomal RNA genes, and four large clusters on chromosomes 8 and 9 harbored 23 rRNA genes (<span class="stix">∼</span>23%). We identified 704 tRNA genes, compared to 611 in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d30e1643">37</a></sup>. We observed large clusters of tRNA genes on chromosomes 1, 7, 8 and 9 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). One hundred fifty-nine microRNA (miRNA) genes and 99 small nuclear RNA (snRNA) genes also showed biased chromosomal distribution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Evolution of foxtail millet</h3><p>The draft genome of foxtail millet was compared to <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice, sorghum and maize. The phylogenetic relationships among the grasses are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig1">Figure 1a</a>. Foxtail millet was split from sorghum and maize <span class="stix">∼</span>27 Myr ago. Paralogous relationships among the nine foxtail millet chromosomes revealed five major duplications between chromosomes 2 and 9, 4 and 1, 7 and 1, 6 and 2, and 5 and 3 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). The accumulated transversion rate at fourfold degenerate sites of the third codons (4DTv) distribution of all paralogous genes further suggested that most of the duplications were generated in the whole genome duplication (WGD) event shared by all grasses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>), which was estimated to be <span class="stix">∼</span>70 Myr ago. Therefore WGD in foxtail millet preceded before the split from sorghum and maize.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genome evolution of foxtail millet."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Genome evolution of foxtail millet.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2195/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2195/MediaObjects/41587_2012_Article_BFnbt2195_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2195/MediaObjects/41587_2012_Article_BFnbt2195_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="289"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Evolutionary relationships of the three subfamilies of grasses, including divergence times, whole genome duplication (WGD) events and nested chromosomes fusion (NCF) events. WGD and NCF events were reported in a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Murat, F. et al. Ancestral grass karyotype reconstruction unravels new mechanisms of genome shuffling as a source of plant evolution. Genome Res. 20, 1545–1557 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d30e1688">29</a></sup>. (<b>b</b>) Age distribution of 4DTv for genes from five grass genomes. A shared paleopolyploidy event was assigned to the peak (4DTv = 0.38). (<b>c</b>) Syntenic blocks between foxtail millet and other sequenced grass genomes, including maize, rice, <i>Brachypodium</i> and sorghum. (<b>d</b>) Shared and unique gene families.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2195/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Intergenomic analyses between foxtail millet, <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice, sorghum and maize revealed highly conserved colinearity, which supports a close evolutionary relationship among these grasses. In total, we identified 24 large colinear blocks between foxtail millet and rice; 19 between foxtail millet and sorghum; 20 between foxtail millet and <i>Brachypodium</i>; and 29 between foxtail millet and maize, indicating that 71.8%, 72.1%, 61.5% and 86.7% of the foxtail millet genome is colinear with these grasses, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 19</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Evolution of chromosomes</h3><p>Despite the close evolutionary relationship among the grasses, the chromosomes of grasses have undergone extensive rearrangements. Ancestral chromosome reconstruction of grasses has revealed that the ancestor of grasses had 12 chromosomes after one WGD and two nest chromosome fission events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Salse, J. et al. Identification and characterization of shared duplications between rice and wheat provide new insight into grass genome evolution. Plant Cell 20, 11–24 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d30e1736">38</a></sup>. However, how different species evolved from the 12 ancestral chromosomes is unknown. To investigate evolution of foxtail millet chromosomes, we used rice as the reference comparison strain because it has retained 12 chromosomes. We found that foxtail millet chromosomes 2, 3 and 9 were colinear with rice chromosomes 7 and 9, 5 and 12, and 3 and 10, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>), indicating that after divergence from the common ancestor, three pairs of chromosomes separately fused to form three chromosomes in foxtail millet. Among the three nest chromosome fusion events that occurred in foxtail millet, two also occurred during the evolution of sorghum (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>), indicating that these two nest chromosome fusion events most likely occurred before the divergence of sorghum and foxtail millet. The nest chromosome fusion event that fused chromosomes 8 and 9 of sorghum into chromosome 3 of foxtail millet likely occurred after divergence of foxtail millet from sorghum. Such chromosome reshuffling events might have introduced genetic variation and contributed to speciation of these plants.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Evolution of gene families</h3><p>Differences in gene families of grass genomes are important sources of genetic traits and adaptation in different species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d30e1754">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d30e1757">40</a></sup>. Since divergence from their common ancestor, grass genomes have an active history of rearrangements that have been accompanied by both minor- or macro-colinearity erosion, which could have a significant impact on gene inventories. In foxtail millet, many genes that underwent ancestral duplication in the grass paleopolyploidy event have since lost one copy. We identified 26,369 (69.5%), 24,722 (65.2%), 25,717 (67.8%), 12,167 (32.1%) foxtail millet genes that were in colinear blocks with rice, sorghum, <i>Brachypodium</i> and maize genes, respectively. Genes in colinear blocks should have paralogs that arose during the WGD. However, within those colinear blocks, 37.1% of rice, 34.7% of sorghum, 34.8% of <i>Brachypodium</i> and 29.9% of maize genes retained only one copy. This indicates that nearly half of the duplicated gene pairs lost one copy over time. The other half of the duplicated genes retained both copies, and would be expected to be subjected to neofunctionalization or subfunctionalization.</p><p>Expansion or contraction of gene families has an important role in diversification of flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Purugganan, M.D., Rounsley, S.D., Schmidt, R.J. &amp; Yanofsky, M.F. Molecular evolution of flower development: diversification of the plant MADS-box regulatory gene family. Genetics 140, 345–356 (1995)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d30e1770">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Lashbrook, C.C., Tieman, D.M. &amp; Klee, H.J. Differential regulation of the tomato ETR gene family throughout plant development. Plant J. 15, 243–252 (1998)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d30e1773">42</a></sup>. We identified gene families in fifteen plants, including plants from the rosids clade (<i>Arabidopsis</i>, papaya, populus, cucumber, soybean, apple, grape, castor bean), asterids clade (potato) and poaceae family (foxtail millet, rice, maize, sorghum, <i>Brachypodium</i>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>). In total, we identified 15,382 plant gene families shared by all fifteen species and 11,088 pan-grass gene families that are shared by all angiosperm plants, most of which were involved in molecular binding, transport, catalytic activity and metabolic processes. Comparing species from poaceae with species from other grass clades, we identified 10,263 gene families specific to poaceae. Additional comparisons within poaceae species revealed 3,552 panicoideae-specific families and 1,517 foxtail millet–specific gene families. Gene Ontology annotation of these foxtail millet–specific gene families showed that some of these gene families may have roles in stress responses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 20</a>). One such family that contains 586 genes was functionally annotated as “response to water.” This indicates that foxtail millet–specific genes might be related to adaptation of foxtail millet to semi-arid environments. In addition to common or specific gene families, we also identified 591 gene families in foxtail millet that showed considerable contraction in foxtail millet compared to other grasses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 21</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">C<sub>4</sub> pathway in grasses</h3><p>C<sub>4</sub> plants have an advantage over C<sub>3</sub> plants because C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis has more efficient carbon fixation, especially in arid areas<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Xu, X. et al. The genomic sequence of the Chinese hamster ovary (CHO)-K1 cell line. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 735–741 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d30e1810">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sage, R.F. &amp; Monson, R.K. C4 Plant Biology (Academic Press, 1999)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d30e1813">43</a></sup>. C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis is of polyphyletic origin, which indicates that relatively few evolutionary changes are required to establish this photosynthetic system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Westhoff, P. &amp; Gowik, U. Evolution of C4 photosynthesis–looking for the master switch. Plant Physiol. 154, 598–601 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d30e1820">44</a></sup>. We identified the key photosynthetic genes involved in the C<sub>4</sub> metabolic cycle (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>) in <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice, sorghum, maize and foxtail millet using known C<sub>4</sub> pathway genes. We found that all the genes involved in C<sub>4</sub> carbon fixation pathways were also present in C<sub>3</sub> plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 22</a>). Thus the evolution of the C<sub>4</sub> pathway may have resulted from functional changes in these genes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="The C4 pathway in grass genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: The C<sub>4</sub> pathway in grass genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2195/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2195/MediaObjects/41587_2012_Article_BFnbt2195_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2195/MediaObjects/41587_2012_Article_BFnbt2195_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="342"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) A schematic representation of the C<sub>4</sub> pathway: carbonic anhydrase is a key gene in the first reaction of the C<sub>4</sub> metabolic cycle. (<b>b</b>) Three subfamilies of the carbonic anhydrase gene family are present in grasses: CAα, CAβ and CAγ. (<b>c</b>) 4DTv distribution for carbonic anhydrase subfamilies. (<b>d</b>) CAα and CAβ genes were all located in a single colinear block for each grass and tandem duplication of CAβ genes were found in C<sub>4</sub> grasses.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2195/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also analyzed the carbonic anhydrase genes, which have important roles in the CO<sub>2</sub>-concentrating process of C<sub>4</sub> plants. A phylogenetic tree of the carbonic anhydrase homologs identified in five grasses revealed that there were three major carbonic anhydrase subfamilies (CAα, CAβ and CAγ), consistent with previous data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ludwig, M. Carbonic anhydrase and the molecular evolution of C4 photosynthesis. Plant Cell Environ. 35, 22–37 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d30e1893">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Tiwari, A., Kumar, P., Singh, S. &amp; Ansari, S. Carbonic anhydrase in relation to higher plants. Photosynthetica 43, 1–11 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d30e1896">45</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). There was one copy of <i>CAα and CAγ</i> in each of the five species, but there were more copies of <i>CAβ</i> in the C<sub>4</sub> plants than in rice and <i>Brachypodium</i>. Distribution of 4DTv of all the identified carbonic anhydrase homolog genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>) suggested that CAγ genes are more ancient than genes from the other two subfamilies and CAα genes were probably generated in the WGD of grasses (4DTv of CAα was 0.38, coincident with the 4DTv of the WGD). CAβ genes were likely to be generated by tandem duplication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>). We identified one CAα gene, two CAβ genes and one CAγ in foxtail millet. The CAα and CAβ genes formed a gene cluster on chromosome 5 (<span class="stix">∼</span>27 Mb) in a colinear block with the homologous counterparts from <i>Brachypodium</i> (chromosome 2, <span class="stix">∼</span>45.25 Mb), rice (chromosome 1, <span class="stix">∼</span>26.5 Mb), sorghum (chromosome 3, <span class="stix">∼</span>57.3 Mb) and maize (chromosomes 3 and 8). Of the three carbonic anhydrase subfamilies, CAβ catalyzes the first reaction in the C<sub>4</sub> pathway by hydrating atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> to bicarbonate in the cytosol of mesophyll cells. Tandem duplication of CAβ genes was only observed in C<sub>4</sub> grasses, so it may be important in the evolution of the C<sub>4</sub> pathway.</p><p>Combining this phylogenetic analysis with the transcriptome data generated in this study, we found that one of the foxtail millet CAβ genes, <i>Ft_CA1</i> (Millet_GLEAN_10030850), was highly expressed in mesophyll (with fragments per kilobase of exon per million fragments mapped, FPKM value 22,970). The nonsynonymous to synonymous ratio of this gene (9.9) revealed that it was under adaptive selection (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). These findings indicate that this <i>Ft_CA1</i> gene might be important for the C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis pathway in foxtail millet.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Exploitation of the foxtail millet genome</h3><p>Availability of a genome sequence facilitates quantitative trait mapping. We used the foxtail millet genome to map herbicide resistance. The Zhang gu strain is resistant to sethoxydim whereas the A2 strain is sensitive to sethoxydim (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). In the F<sub>2</sub> population of Zhang gu and A2 that was used for genetic map construction, the segregation of resistance to sethoxydim compared with sensitivity to sethoxydim was about 3:1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 23</a>), which suggested that sethoxydim resistance was probably related to a single, completely dominant, nuclear gene. We were able to map the trait to one 1.1-cM (2.4-Mb) region between marker SIsv0367 and marker SIsv1223 using our genetic map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). To fine-map this trait, we chose five SNP markers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 24</a>) located between SIsv0367 and SIsv1223, and genotyped them in the F<sub>2</sub> population. The trait of sethoxydim resistance was located between SNP markers 2 and 5 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>), in a <span class="stix">∼</span>100-kb region. Four genes were located in that region, including <i>Ft_SR1</i>, Millet_GLEAN_10024326, which was annotated as acetyl-CoA carboxylase. Acetyl-CoA carboxylase is the target enzyme of sethoxydim, and mutations in this gene would result in resistance to sethoxydim<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Délye, C., Wang, T. &amp; Darmency, H. An isoleucine-leucine substitution in chloroplastic acetyl-CoA carboxylase from green foxtail (Setaria viridis L. Beauv.) is responsible for resistance to the cyclohexanedione herbicide sethoxydim. Planta 214, 421–427 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d30e1978">15</a></sup>. Within this gene, there were only six SNPs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). Of those SNPs, one possible functional SNP was located in the coding sequence of this gene and caused a codon change from CTA (Zhang gu) to ATA (A2) which changed leucine (Leu, in Zhang gu) to isoleucine (Ile, in A2). In green millet, the substitution from Ile to Leu in one acetyl-CoA gene has already been shown to code for sethoxydim resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Délye, C., Wang, T. &amp; Darmency, H. An isoleucine-leucine substitution in chloroplastic acetyl-CoA carboxylase from green foxtail (Setaria viridis L. Beauv.) is responsible for resistance to the cyclohexanedione herbicide sethoxydim. Planta 214, 421–427 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d30e1985">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wang, T. &amp; Darmency, H. Inheritance of sethoxydim resistance in foxtail millet, Setaria italica (L.) Beauv. Euphytica 94, 69–73 (1997)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d30e1988">46</a></sup>. Therefore, we have shown that the draft genome sequence can be used to accurately identify the gene for sethoxydim resistance.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Mapping sethoxydim resistance."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Mapping sethoxydim resistance.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2195/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2195/MediaObjects/41587_2012_Article_BFnbt2195_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2195/MediaObjects/41587_2012_Article_BFnbt2195_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="447"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Sethoxydim resistance of Zhang gu compared with the sethoxydim sensitive phenotype of A2. (<b>b</b>) Mapping of the trait by structural variant markers as well as fine mapping using SNPs. (<b>c</b>) Variations located within the acetyl-CoA gene (Millet_GLEAN_10024326) between Zhang gu and A2. The fine mapping of the sethoxydim trait was consistent with previous studies of this trait in green millet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Délye, C., Wang, T. &amp; Darmency, H. An isoleucine-leucine substitution in chloroplastic acetyl-CoA carboxylase from green foxtail (Setaria viridis L. Beauv.) is responsible for resistance to the cyclohexanedione herbicide sethoxydim. Planta 214, 421–427 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d30e2014">15</a></sup>, in which experimental validation of the gene function was also carried out.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2195/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><p>The genetic and genomic data of economically important grasses are an invaluable resource for comparative and functional genomics studies. We have added to the resource of grass genomes with the draft genome sequence of foxtail millet. To assemble the foxtail millet genome we applied a whole genome shotgun strategy combined with next-generation sequencing technology. Foxtail millet has a small genome (<span class="stix">∼</span>490 M), which is rich in repeats, consistent with the sequenced genomes of other grass species, and low in heterozygosity. We assembled the genome using data from next-generation sequencers and mapped the sequences onto chromosomes using a genetic map. The genome assembly covers <span class="stix">∼</span>86% of the estimated genome size, and the unassembled part is largely composed of repeat elements. Repeat-rich regions of the genome, especially long repeats that cannot be resolved using large insert-size libraries, are difficult to assemble, which resulted in a large number of contigs and scaffolds. However, for regions lacking repeat sequences, the assembly was easier and most of the genome was present in just a few hundred long scaffolds. This draft genome will be improved if bacterial artificial chromosome sequencing data of foxtail millet is developed.</p><p>The draft genome of foxtail millet will provide an important resource for crop improvement. The provision of a set of genetic markers through resequencing of a second foxtail millet strain (A2) enabled us to develop a genetic map. We also generated a set of RNA data from different tissues to underpin future studies of gene function. Finally, we applied the genome sequence to map an herbicide-resistance trait, to demonstrate the utility of this draft genome in crop improvement. Combining these genome and transcriptome data with a germplasm collection, gene transformation method and other available resources, foxtail millet can now be studied and improved in an effective way.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Sample preparation.</h3><p>The widely cultivated commercial line Zhang gu was collected from northern China. The other commercial line A2 was collected from Zhangjiakou, China. The Zhang gu line was herbicide (sethoxydim) resistant. A hybrid population of 480 individuals was bred from these two lines. The F<sub>2</sub> population was used in construction of a genetic map and QTL analysis. Genomic DNA was isolated from young leaves using a modified SDS method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Peng, J., Wang, H., Haley, S.D., Peairs, F.B. &amp; Lapitan, N.L.V. Molecular mapping of the Russian wheat aphid resistance gene in wheat. Crop Sci. 47, 2418–2429 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d30e2052">47</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Library construction.</h3><p>Paired-end libraries with 170 bp, 200 bp, 500 bp, 800 bp, 2 kb, 5 kb, 10 kb and 20 kb, respectively, were constructed following a standard protocol provided by Illumina. We used a fosmid library construction method to construct the 40 kb library (see details in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>, “Construction of 40 kb insert size library,” <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genome sequencing.</h3><p>The sequencing was performed using Illumina Genome Analyzer II and HiSeq 2000 (short insert size libraries were sequenced by GA II and long insert size libraries, 5 k, 10 k, 20 k, 40 k, were sequenced by HiSeq 2000, just for convenience of sequencing and data output) according to the manufacturer's standard protocol. Low-quality reads, reads with adaptor sequences and duplicated reads were filtered, and the remaining high-quality data was used in the assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome assembly.</h3><p>Genome size was estimated by k-mer analysis (see details in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>, “Estimation of the genome size,” <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d30e2092">23</a></sup> was applied to assemble the genome in the procedure of contig construction, scaffold construction and gap closure. Then a genetic map was constructed for anchoring the scaffolds onto chromosomes. We developed genetic markers by resequencing strain A2. We constructed a short insert size library (500 bp) of A2 from genomic DNA of young leaves and Illumina GA II was used to do the sequencing. In total <span class="stix">∼</span>5G raw reads were generated. After mapping these reads back to the scaffolds of the Zhang gu strain, variations were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>). SOAPsnp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Li, R. et al. SNP detection for massively parallel whole-genome resequencing. Genome Res. 19, 1124–1132 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d30e2106">48</a></sup> (Version 1.02) was used to identify SNPs. SOAPindel (<a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/</a>, Version 1.01) was applied to identify indels. SOAPsv (<a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/</a>, Version 1.01) was used to identify structure variations.</p><p>In order to construct the genetic map, we selected 641 structural variants and 118 SNPs in the 613 longest scaffolds. For structural variants, in order to facilitate the genotyping, we selected structural variants with a size change of <span class="stix">∼</span>500 bp, which were determined by PCR and agarose gel electrophoresis. Primers were then designed based on the genome sequences of both structural variant and SNP markers. PCR was used to amplify the corresponding region in 480 F<sub>2</sub> individuals generated by crossing Zhang gu and A2. Genotypes of structural variants were then determined by agarose gel electrophoresis and genotypes of SNPs were determined by Sanger sequencing. We constructed the genetic map using JoinMap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Stam, P. Construction of integrated genetic linkage maps by means of a new computer package: Join Map. Plant J. 3, 739–744 (1993)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d30e2129">49</a></sup>. The genetic map was used to anchor the 631 longest scaffolds onto 9 chromosomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Assessing the genome assembly.</h3><p>To assess the genome assembly, we first mapped all the reads to the assembled genome by SOAP2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Li, R. et al. SNP detection for massively parallel whole-genome resequencing. Genome Res. 19, 1124–1132 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d30e2141">48</a>). Only mapped reads were then used to assess the support of assembly in regions. Then we compared the assembled genome sequence to the genome of sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d30e2145">27</a></sup> by Mummer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d30e2149">50</a></sup> (Version 3.0) and colinear regions were thus identified. Rearrangements were then assessed by the support of paired-end mapped reads.</p><p>Optical mapping was then conducted. Large amounts of DNA from the Zhang gu strain was extracted from the young leaves for construction of optical maps. Whole genome shotgun single-molecule restriction maps were then generated with the highly automated Argus system (OpGen) based on optical mapping technology. Overall, 252,267 single-molecule restriction maps (SMRMs) (&gt;250 kb) with an average size of 271 kb were generated. The total size was about 68.9 Gb, <span class="stix">∼</span>140× depth of the genome. The optical data were mapped to the genome assembly using commercial software (OpGen). Briefly, the fragments of optical data were mapped back to the genome according to the restriction enzyme recognition sites and the length of the fragments. The mapping accuracy of the restriction enzyme recognition sites was then determined by that software.</p><p>ALLPATHS-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513–1518 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d30e2159">24</a></sup> (Release 37250) was then applied to assemble the genome. The assembled large scaffolds by ALLPATHS-LG were compared to our genome assembly by the software, Mummer.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">RNA sequencing.</h3><p>Four tissues (root, stem, leaf and spike) from young seedlings were used to isolate RNA. Total RNA was extracted using Trizol (Invitrogen). The isolated RNA was then treated by RNAse-Free DNase, and then subsequently treated using Illumina mRNA-Seq Prep Kit following the manufacturer's instruction. The insert size of the RNA libraries was about 200 bp, and the sequencing was done using Illumina GA II. Raw reads were filtered if there were adaptor contaminations and low quality (&gt;10% bases with unknown quality). After filtering, we assembled the RNA data by Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Grabherr, M.G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d30e2171">51</a></sup> (r2011-08-20) of four tissues separately.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Assessing the gene region coverage.</h3><p>We assessed the gene region coverage of the genome assembly by using the assembled RNA sequences, core eukaryotic genes, publicly available foxtail gene sequences and incomplete gene fragments in the genome.
