RNA assembly of four tissues was mapped back to the genome assembly using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Kent, W.J. BLAT–the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d30e2183">52</a></sup>. The coverage was then calculated based on the mapping result. Core eukaryotic genes identified by CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d30e2187">32</a></sup> and the foxtail millet gene sequences archived in NCBI nucleotide database (28 genes) were also mapped back to the genome assembly by BLAT to calculate the gene region coverage. Proteins of <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice, maize and sorghum were mapped to foxtail millet genome by tblastn. Gene fragments were thus identified. And we filtered redundant gene fragments and retained fragments longer than 150 bp. The fragments were then subjected to a <i>de novo</i> gene annotation by AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, W309–312 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d30e2197">53</a></sup> to identify the incorrect start codons, premature stop codons and other incorrect codons. In total we identified <span class="stix">∼</span>13,000 gene fragments, and we randomly selected 81 of them to be validated by PCR and Sanger sequencing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Repeat annotation.</h3><p>Transposable elements were identified in the genome using a combination of <i>de novo</i> and homology-based approaches. First, we used three <i>de novo</i> software programs (LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–268 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d30e2215">54</a></sup> (Version 1.0.3), PILER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Edgar, R.C. &amp; Myers, E.W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21 (suppl. 1), i152–i158 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d30e2219">55</a></sup> and RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Price, A.L., Jones, N.C. &amp; Pevzner, P.A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21 (suppl. 1), i351–i358 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d30e2223">56</a></sup> (Version 1.05)) to build <i>de novo</i> repeat database of foxtail millet. We then used RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr Prot. Bioinform. 25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d30e2231">57</a></sup> (Version 3.2.7) to identify repeats using both the repeat database we had built and Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d30e2235">58</a></sup>. We also used RepeatProteinMask (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>, Version 3.2.2) to search the protein database in Repbase against the genome to identify repeat related proteins. We annotated the tandem repeats in the foxtail millet genome using RepeatMasker and TRF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d30e2246">59</a></sup>. Then we combined the <i>de novo</i> prediction, the homolog prediction of transposable elements according to the coordination in the genome. Complete LTRs were predicted by LTR_STRUC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="McCarthy, E.M. &amp; McDonald, J.F. LTR_STRUC: a novel search and identification program for LTR retrotransposons. Bioinformatics 19, 362–367 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d30e2254">60</a></sup>. Muscle was used to align LTRs and divergence was calculated using distmat in EMBOSS package. Divergent time was estimated by setting <i>Brachypodium</i> as reference. The divergent time of LTRs in <i>Brachypodium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d30e2263">33</a></sup> was used.</p><p>Several steps were followed to classify transposable elements: (i) blastn against Repbase, (ii) blastx against transposable element proteins, (iii) blastn against plant/animal repeat databases, (iv) blastx against SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="The UniProt Consortium. The Universal Protein Resource (UniProt) 2009. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D169–D174 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d30e2270">61</a></sup> proteins, (v) tblastx against Repbase and (vi) tblastx against plant/animal repeat databases. For each step, the transposable elements having confident hits with known repeats were classified either at the DNA level (E-value ≤ 10<sup>−10</sup>, identity ≥ 80%, coverage ≥ 30% and the minimal matching length ≥ 80 bp) or at the protein level (E-value ≤ 10<sup>−4</sup>, identity ≥ 30%, coverage ≥ 30% and the minimal matching length ≥ 30 amino acid). LTR retrotransposons identified by LTR_FINDER were classified as “unclassified LTR” if no homology to known repeats was found.</p><p>To infer the insertion time of LTRs, full-length LTRs were identified using LTR_STRUC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="McCarthy, E.M. &amp; McDonald, J.F. LTR_STRUC: a novel search and identification program for LTR retrotransposons. Bioinformatics 19, 362–367 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d30e2281">60</a></sup> with default parameters. The insertion time was estimated based on the sequence divergence between two LTRs of the same element. All the LTRs were aligned by MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Edgar, R.C. MUSCLE: a multiple sequence alignment method with reduced time and space complexity. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 113 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d30e2285">62</a></sup>, and then the distance between them was calculated by the Kimura two-parameter model using distmat program implemented in the EMBOSS package (<a href="http://emboss.sourceforge.net/">http://emboss.sourceforge.net/</a>). Then the same procedure was done to the <i>Brachypodium</i> genome, and according to the insertion time of LTRs in <i>Brachypodium</i> reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d30e2303">33</a></sup>, the insertion time of LTRs in the foxtail millet genome was estimated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Gene prediction.</h3><p>We conducted the gene annotation by the following steps: (i) <i>De novo</i> gene prediction. We performed <i>de novo</i> predictions on repeat masked genome assembly. We used AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, W309–312 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d30e2321">53</a></sup> (Version 2.03) and Fgenesh<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Salamov, A.A. &amp; Solovyev, V.V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d30e2325">63</a></sup> (Version 1.3) to do the <i>de novo</i> annotation. (ii) Homolog prediction. We mapped the protein sequences from <i>Arabidopsis</i>, rice, sorghum and maize to the foxtail millet genome using tblastn, by an E-value cutoff of 10<sup>−5</sup>, and then Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d30e2338">64</a></sup> (Version 2.2.0) was used for gene annotation. (iii) RNA-aided annotation. We mapped all the RNA reads back to the reference genome by Tophat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d30e2342">65</a></sup> (Version 1.0.14, implemented with bowtie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Langmead, B., Trapnell, C., Pop, M. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. Ultrafast and memory-efficient alignment of short DNA sequences to the human genome. Genome Biol. 10, R25 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d30e2346">66</a></sup> Version 0.12.5) and assembled the transcripts according to the genome using Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d30e2350">67</a></sup> (Version 0.8.2). Open reading frames (ORFs) were predicted using BESTORF (<a href="http://linux1.softberry.com/berry.phtml?topic=bestorf&amp;group=programs&amp;subgroup=gfind">http://linux1.softberry.com/berry.phtml?topic=bestorf&amp;group=programs&amp;subgroup=gfind</a>) with parameters trained on monocot genes without filtering of UTRs. (iv) GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d30e2362">68</a></sup>. All the predictions were combined using GLEAN to produce the consensus gene sets.</p><p>Gene functions were assigned according to the best match of the alignments using blastp (E-value ≤ 10<sup>−5</sup>) searching against SwissProt and Uniprot databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="The UniProt Consortium. The Universal Protein Resource (UniProt) 2009. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D169–D174 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d30e2371">61</a></sup> (Release 15.10). The motifs and domains of genes were determined by InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W116–W120 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d30e2375">69</a></sup> (Version 4.5) against protein databases. Gene Ontology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. The Gene Ontology Consortium. Nat. Genet. 25, 25–29 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d30e2379">70</a></sup> IDs for each gene were obtained from the corresponding InterPro entry. All genes were aligned against KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27–30 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d30e2383">71</a></sup> (Release 48.2) proteins, and the pathway in which the gene might be involved was derived. If the best hit of the genes in any of these process was “function unknown”, “putative”, the second-best hits were used to assign function until there were no more hits that meet the alignment criteria (then this gene is determined as functionally unknown).</p><p>The tRNA genes were identified by tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Lowe, T.M. &amp; Eddy, S.R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d30e2390">72</a></sup>. For rRNA identification, we first downloaded the <i>Arabidopsis</i> rRNA sequences from NCBI (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/guide/dna-rna/">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/guide/dna-rna/</a>). Then rRNAs in the database were aligned against the foxtail millet genome using blastn to identify possible rRNAs. Other noncoding RNAs, including miRNA, snRNA, were identified using INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Nawrocki, E.P., Kolbe, D.L. &amp; Eddy, S.R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335–1337 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d30e2404">73</a></sup> by searching against the Rfam database.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Evolution analysis.</h3><p>We first identified homologous gene families across all the available grass genomes (<i>Brachypodium</i>, rice, maize, sorghum and foxtail millet) using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J., Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d30e2419">74</a></sup>. Gene families for which there is a single gene in each species (single-copy gene families) were then extracted. For each of these single- copy gene families, proteins of the homolog genes were aligned by muscle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Edgar, R.C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d30e2423">75</a></sup> and the alignments were CDS-back-translated into CDS using an in-house perl script. Then all the back translated CDS sequences were joined into one super gene for each species. Fourfold generation sites were then identified in the super genes of all five species and the transversion rates (4DTv) between every two species were then calculated by an in-house perl script. Then using the 4DTv to represent distances between every two species we applied MrBayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Huelsenbeck, J.P. &amp; Ronquist, F. MRBAYES: Bayesian inference of phylogenetic trees. Bioinformatics 17, 754–755 (2001)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d30e2427">76</a></sup> (Version 3.2.0) to construct a phylogenetic tree. Finally MultiDivergence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Thorne, J.L. &amp; Kishino, H. Divergence time and evolutionary rate estimation with multilocus data. Syst. Biol. 51, 689–702 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d30e2431">77</a></sup> was applied to infer the divergence time base on the phylogenetic tree constructed (setting the divergence time of rice and <i>Brachypodium</i> to 34 Myr ago in previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d30e2439">33</a></sup>). The topology was simplified into a sketch map in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#Fig1">Figure 1</a> with wheat added. The estimated divergent time was assigned onto branches.</p><p>To identify syntenic blocks within foxtail millet genome, we used MCscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944–1954 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d30e2449">78</a></sup>, which set genes as anchors. First we used blastp to align foxtail millet protein data set to itself (setting E-value to be 10<sup>−7</sup>). The alignment result was then subjected to MCScan to determine syntenic block. For gene pairs in the syntenic blocks identified by MCscan, we calculated the 4DTv of each gene pair and plotted the distribution of the 4DTv of all the gene pairs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Identification of chromosome reshuffling.</h3><p>After identification of the syntenic and colinear blocks between foxtail millet with other grasses, we set rice genome as reference and identified all the homologous chromosome relationships between rice and foxtail millet, rice and sorghum, foxtail millet and sorghum, foxtail millet and maize.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Gene family analysis.</h3><p>We clustered paralogs and orthologs using OrthoMCL method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J., Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.2195#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d30e2472">74</a></sup> with the default settings (blastp E-value ≤ 10<sup>−5</sup>) for 15 sequenced plants, including eudicots, asteroids and grasses. After getting all gene families, we classified the families according to the presence or absence of genes for specific species and determined which gene families were species-specific or genus-specific.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Identification of genes in C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis pathway.</h3><p>We retrieved the photosynthesis pathway genes from two resources: (i) from the NCBI database (we downloaded the genes identified to be involved in photosynthesis of sorghum, maize and rice); and (ii) from the functional annotation of foxtail millet proteins.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Trait mapping of sethoxydim.</h3><p>We used the F<sub>2</sub> populations (480 individuals) generated by crossing between the Zhang gu (sethoxydim resistant) and A2 (sethoxydim sensitive) strains. Sethoxydim-resistant phenotype data were collected in the F<sub>2</sub> population by seeding on sethoxydim medium (50 mg/l). The seeds of F<sub>2</sub> plants were grown in the sethoxydim medium to determine the phenotypes. If seeds were resistant to sethoxydim, the phenotype of the F<sub>2</sub> individual was resistant to sethoxydim. If the seeds were segregated in terms of resistance to sethoxydim, the phenotype of the F<sub>2</sub> individual was heterozygous.</p><p>All the in-house scripts used are available on request.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Data access</h3><p>The genomic reads of Zhang gu and A2, as well as RNA sequencing data of Zhang gu, have been deposited into NCBI Short Read Archive (SRA) under accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA048234">SRA048234</a>. The information for the raw reads data can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 25</a>. The genome sequence and annotation data set have been deposited into NCBI (Project ID: PRJNA77795 (chromosomes) and PRJNA73995 (scaffolds), the accession number is <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=AGTC01000000">AGTC01000000</a>). The genome assembly, annotation as well as other data mentioned can be downloaded from <a href="ftp://ftp.genomics.org.cn/pub/Foxtail_millet">ftp://ftp.genomics.org.cn/pub/Foxtail_millet</a>. Genome browser can be found on the foxtail millet genome website (<a href="http://foxtailmillet.genomics.org.cn/">http://foxtailmillet.genomics.org.cn/</a>). The primer sequence of 759 markers used to construct the genetic map can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 3</a>. The phenotype data of 480 F<sub>2</sub> individuals are in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 24</a>. The additional genetic markers can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2195#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 25</a>.</p></div></div></section>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2Fs00438-002-0728-8" aria-label="View reference 9">Article</a> 
    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 9 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=Structural%20variation%20in%20the%20Waxy%20gene%20and%20differentiation%20in%20foxtail%20millet%20%5BSetaria%20italica%20%28L.%29%20P.%20Beauv.%5D%3A%20implications%20for%20multiple%20origins%20of%20the%20waxy%20phenotype&amp;journal=Mol.%20Genet.%20Genomics&amp;volume=268&amp;pages=214-222&amp;publication_year=2002&amp;author=Fukunaga%2CKF&amp;author=Kawase%2CMK&amp;author=Kato%2CKK">

Jun W., Jian W., G.Z., X.L., X.X., Junyi W. and Y.L. led the sequencing and analysis. Z.Y., S.P., Q.X. and X.P. did the genome assembly. M.X., X.Y. and D.Z. did the annotation. Z.Q., R.W., T.G., Y.C., P.H., C.L., H.X., Q.S. and Q.C. conducted the trait mapping analysis. X.L., S.C., Z.Q., X.X. and G.Z. wrote the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nbt.2195/email/correspondent/c1/new">Jun Wang</a> or <a id="corresp-c2" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nbt.2195/email/correspondent/c2/new">Zhihai Zhao</a> or <a id="corresp-c3" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nbt.2195/email/correspondent/c3/new">Jian Wang</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>The authors declare no competing financial interests.</p>
